            <meta name="dc.description" content="The p38 family is a highly evolutionarily conserved group of mitogen-activated protein kinases (MAPKs) that is involved in and helps co-ordinate cellular responses to nearly all stressful stimuli. This review provides a succinct summary of multiple aspects of the biology, role, and substrates of the mammalian family of p38 kinases. Since p38 activity is implicated in inflammatory and other diseases, we also discuss the clinical implications and pharmaceutical approaches to inhibit p38.">

<button class="tertiary grey float-right" data-scrollto=article-reports>DETAILS</button> <div class="status-row referee-reports-container"> REVIEWER STATUS <span class=status-icons> <span data-refInfo=69529-65076></span> </span> </div> </div> </div> </div> <h2 class="article-headings article-page-abstract" id=anchor-abstract> <span class="f1r-article-mobile-inline abstract-heading-border"></span> Abstract </h2> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout"> <div class="abstract-text is-expanded"> The p38 family is a highly evolutionarily conserved group of mitogen-activated protein kinases (MAPKs) that is involved in and helps co-ordinate cellular responses to nearly all stressful stimuli. This review provides a succinct summary of multiple aspects of the biology, role, and substrates of the mammalian family of p38 kinases. Since p38 activity is implicated in inflammatory and other diseases, we also discuss the clinical implications and pharmaceutical approaches to inhibit p38. </div> <div class=abstract-for-mobile> <div class="margin-top-30 padding-bottom-30 research-layout is-centered"> <button class="primary orange-text white-bg bigger-text abstract-expand-button-mobile with-border show" style="display: none;"> LESS <span class="f1r-icon icon-10_less_small orange vmiddle big"></span> </button> </div> </div> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div class="article-context no-divider"> <div class="article-abstract article-page-general-text-mobile research-layout generated-article-body"> <h2 class=main-title>Keywords</h2> <p class="u-mb--0 u-pb--2"> p38, MAPK, inflammation, signalling </p> </div> </div> <div class=article-information> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom"> <div id=corresponding-author-icon class="email-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-6_email orange"></span> <div id=corresponding-author-window class="margin-top-20 popup-window-wrapper is-hidden"> <div class="popup-window corresponding-authors-popup"> <div class=corresponding-author-container> <div class="popup-window-title small">Corresponding Author(s)</div> <div class=authors> Jiahuai Han (<a href="mailto:jiahuaihan@xmu.edu.cn">jiahuaihan@xmu.edu.cn</a>) <br> John Silke (<a href="mailto:silke@wehi.edu.au">silke@wehi.edu.au</a>) </div> </div> <div class="margin-top margin-bottom float-left"> <button id=close-popup-window class=general-white-orange-button>Close</button> </div> </div> </div> </div> <span class="icon-text float-left" data-test-id=box-corresponding-author> <b>Corresponding authors:</b> Jiahuai Han, John Silke </span> <div class=clearfix></div> </span> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom competing-interests-display"> <span class=competing-interests-title>Competing interests:</span> No competing interests were disclosed. </span> <div class="info-separation padding-bottom grant-information-display"> <span </div> <span class="f1r-article-desk info-separation padding-bottom"> <span class="copywrite-icon float-left"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-100_open_access"></span> </span> <span class="icon-text This is an open access article distributed under the terms of the <a href="http://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" target=_blank data-test-id=box-licence-link>Creative Commons Attribution License</a>, which permits unrestricted use, distribution, and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited. </span> <div class=clearfix></div> </span> <span class="info-separation padding-bottom" data-test-id=box-how-to-cite> <b>How to cite:</b> <span class="article-title-and-info in-article-box"> Han J, Wu J and Silke J. An overview of mammalian p38 mitogen-activated protein kinases, central regulators of cell stress and receptor signaling [version 1; peer review: 2 approved]</span>. <i>F1000Research</i> 2020, <b>9</b>(F1000 Faculty Rev):653 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.22092.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.22092.1</a>) </span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-first-published><b>First published:</b> 29 Jun 2020, <b>9</b>(F1000 Faculty Rev):653 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.22092.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.22092.1</a>)</span> <span class=info-separation data-test-id=box-latest-published><b>Latest published:</b> 29 Jun 2020, <b>9</b>(F1000 Faculty Rev):653 (<a href="https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.22092.1" target=_blank>https://doi.org/10.12688/f1000research.22092.1</a>)</span> </div> <div class=clearfix></div> <div id=article-context class=article-context> <div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d15573e179>p38 mitogen-activated protein kinases</h2><p class="" id=d15573e182>p38 (originally named p38) was identified and cloned as a 38 kDa protein that was tyrosine-phosphorylated in response to LPS stimulation in mammalian cells<sup><a href="#ref-1">1</a>,<a href="#ref-2">2</a></sup>. Sequence comparison, on the day p38 was cloned, revealed that it belonged to the mitogen-activated protein kinase (MAPK) family and that a <i>Saccharomyces cerevisiae</i> osmotic response protein kinase HOG1 was a p38 homologue<sup><a href="#ref-3">3</a><a href="#ref-5">5</a></sup>. p38 was also named cytokine suppressive drug binding protein (CSBP) because it was identified as the target of a series of anti-inflammatory pyridinyl-imidazole compounds and as reactivating kinase (RK) because it phosphorylated and activated MK2<sup><a href="#ref-3">3</a><a href="#ref-5">5</a></sup>. There are four members of the p38 group of MAPKs encoded by four different genes in mammals: p38 (<i>MAPK14</i>, chromosome 6p21.31 in humans), p38 (<i>MAPK11</i>, SAPK2b, Chr22q13.33)<sup><a href="#ref-6">6</a></sup>, p38 (<i>MAPK12</i>, ERK6, SAPK3, Chr22q13.33)<sup><a href="#ref-7">7</a>,<a href="#ref-8">8</a></sup>, and p38 (<i>MAPK13</i>, SAPK4, Serk4, Chr6p21.31)<sup><a href="#ref-9">9</a>,<a href="#ref-10">10</a></sup>. As can be surmised from their chromosomal locations, <i>MAPK14</i>/p38 and <i>MAPK13</i>/p38 are physically close and separated by just over 15 kb, as are <i>MAPK12</i>/p38 and <i>MAPK11</i>/p38, which are separated by less than 2 kb. All the p38s contain a conserved ThrGlyTyr (TGY) dual phosphorylation motif within the kinase activation loop, and both Thr and Tyr phosphorylation are necessary to fully activate the kinase<sup><a href="#ref-11">11</a></sup>. However, monophosphorylated p38 Thr<sup>180</sup> has some kinase activity <i>in vitro</i>, but a different substrate specificity, when compared with dual-site phosphorylated p38<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup>. p38 group members are expressed ubiquitously, but p38 and p38 are enriched in certain cell types and tissues, such as p38 in skeletal muscle and p38 in the salivary, pituitary, and adrenal glands<sup><a href="#ref-13">13</a></sup>. p38 shares more amino acid sequence identity with p38 (~70%), while p38 and p38 share ~60% identity with p38. p38 and p38 also share high sequence homology with cyclin-dependent kinases (CDKs) and are sensitive to some CDK inhibitors<sup><a href="#ref-14">14</a></sup>.</p></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d15573e279>Activation and inactivation of p38</h2><p class="" id=d15573e282>p38 is involved in the response to almost all stressful stimuli, including LPS, UV light, heat shock, osmotic shock, inflammatory cytokines, T cell receptor ligation, glucose starvation, and oncogene activation<sup><a href="#ref-2">2</a>,<a href="#ref-4">4</a>,<a href="#ref-5">5</a>,<a href="#ref-15">15</a><a href="#ref-20">20</a></sup>. Under certain circumstances, it is also activated upon growth factor stimulation. It should be noted that the activation of p38 in some cases is cell type specific, since an activating stimulus in one cell type may inhibit p38 in other cell types<sup><a href="#ref-21">21</a></sup>. The study of p38 group members other than p38 has been less intensive; however, where it has been examined, the other p38s are frequently co-activated with p38<sup><a href="#ref-22">22</a></sup>.</p><p class="" id=d15573e309>Like other MAPK signaling pathways, the activation of all p38s is mediated by a kinase cascade: MAPKKK (MAP3K), which activates MAPKK (MAP2K), which in turn activates MAPK. The MAP2K kinases MKK3 and MKK6 are the major upstream kinases for p38 activation<sup><a href="#ref-23">23</a><a href="#ref-25">25</a></sup>. Although MKK3 and MKK6 phosphorylate most p38 isoforms <i>in vitro</i>, selective activation and substrate specificity have been observed <i>in vivo</i><sup><a href="#ref-26">26</a></sup>. MKK4 has also been reported to phosphorylate p38 and p38 in specific cell types<sup><a href="#ref-9">9</a></sup>. A number of MAP3Ks have been reported to participate in p38 activation including TAK1<sup><a href="#ref-27">27</a></sup>, ASK1<sup><a href="#ref-28">28</a></sup>, DLK<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a></sup>, and MEKK4<sup><a href="#ref-29">29</a>,<a href="#ref-30">30</a></sup>. Low-molecular-weight GTP-binding proteins in the Rho family, such as Rac1 and Cdc42, can activate p38 through binding to MEK1 or MLK1, which function as upstream activators of MAP3K<sup><a href="#ref-31">31</a>,<a href="#ref-32">32</a></sup>.</p><p class="" id=d15573e359>p38 can also be activated by MAP2K-independent mechanisms. TAB1 (TAK1-binding protein 1) directly interacts with p38 and can promote trans autophosphorylation on Thr<sup>180</sup> and Tyr<sup>182</sup> and thus full activation of p38<sup><a href="#ref-33">33</a></sup>. A subsequent study revealed that autophosphorylation of Thr<sup>180</sup> and Tyr<sup>182</sup> requires a conserved Thr<sup>185</sup> residue<sup><a href="#ref-34">34</a></sup>. TAB1-dependent p38 activation has been implicated in ischemic myocardial injury and T cell anergy<sup><a href="#ref-35">35</a>,<a href="#ref-36">36</a></sup>. TAB1 is also claimed to play a role in Sestrin-mediated p38 activation<sup><a href="#ref-12">12</a></sup>. Another MAP2K-independent activation is mediated by ZAP70 after T cell receptor ligation. ZAP70 can directly phosphorylate p38/ on Tyr<sup>323</sup><sup><a href="#ref-18">18</a></sup>, leading to autophosphorylation on Thr<sup>180</sup>, one of the dual phosphorylation sites. As discussed, mono-Thr<sup>180</sup> phosphorylated p38 still has some kinase activity<sup><a href="#ref-37">37</a></sup>, and loss of ZAP70-mediated p38 activation in p38<sup>Y323F</sup> double knock-in mice reduces autoimmunity and inflammation in several autoimmune disease models<sup><a href="#ref-38">38</a><a href="#ref-40">40</a></sup>. Interestingly, p38 also phosphorylates ZAP70, resulting in a decrease in the size and persistence of the T cell receptor signaling complex, and therefore acts as a feedback regulator of ZAP70<sup><a href="#ref-41">41</a></sup>.</p><p class="" id=d15573e428>Conversely, de-phosphorylation of both threonine and tyrosine residues in the activation loop inactivates MAPKs, and this is mainly carried out by dual-specificity phosphatases of the MAPK phosphatase (MKP)/dual specificity phosphatase (DUSP) family<sup><a href="#ref-42">42</a></sup>. Although several MKPs have been reported to dephosphorylate p38, MKP1/DUSP1, MKP5/DUSP10, MKP8/DUSP26, and DUSP8 are more potent inhibitors of p38 and JNK than ERK<sup><a href="#ref-43">43</a></sup>. A recent report showed that DUSP12 is also a p38 phosphatase<sup><a href="#ref-44">44</a></sup>. While there are a number of p38 DUSPs, no DUSP for p38 or p38 has been reported, and these two p38s are resistant to several known p38 MKPs such as MKP1, 3, 5, and 7<sup><a href="#ref-45">45</a></sup>. p38-dependent upregulation of MKP1 was reported and is believed to be part of a negative feedback loop of p38 activation<sup><a href="#ref-46">46</a></sup>. Other types of phosphatases have also been reported to target p38 MAPKs, such as CacyBP/SIP<sup><a href="#ref-47">47</a></sup>, Wip1<sup><a href="#ref-48">48</a></sup>, and PP2C<sup><a href="#ref-49">49</a>,<a href="#ref-50">50</a></sup>. The substrate specificity between p38 and phosphatases and the related physiological functions <i>in vivo</i> still need further investigation. p38 has also been reported to be degraded by a p38/JNK/ubiquitin-proteasome-dependent pathway, which represents an additional mechanism by which p38 kinases may cross regulate each other<sup><a href="#ref-51">51</a></sup>. Yet other ways of regulating p38 are suggested from studies in <i>Caenorhabditis elegans</i>, where a genetic screen for resistance against bacterial infection identified RIOK-1, an atypical serine kinase and human RIO kinase homolog, as a suppressor of the p38 pathway<sup><a href="#ref-52">52</a></sup>. As RIOK-1 is a transcriptional target of the p38 pathway in <i>C. elegans</i>, this suggests that RIOK-1 is part of a negative feedback loop. A brief summary of the p38 pathway is shown in <a href="#f1">Figure 1</a>.</p><a name=f1 class=n-a></a><div class="fig panel clearfix"><a target=_blank href="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24364/2a3116c1-c38e-4e20-ab4b-f97903684b74_figure1.gif"><img alt="2a3116c1-c38e-4e20-ab4b-f97903684b74_figure1.gif" src="https://f1000researchdata.s3.amazonaws.com/manuscripts/24364/2a3116c1-c38e-4e20-ab4b-f97903684b74_figure1.gif"></a><div class=caption><h3>Figure 1. A diagram of the p38 pathway.</h3><p id=d15573e499>MKP, mitogen-activated protein kinase phosphatase; TAB1, TAK1-binding protein 1; Tyr, tyrosine.</p></div></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d15573e509>Downstream substrates of p38</h2><div class=section><a name=d15573e512 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Protein kinases</h3><p class="" id=d15573e517>The p38 MAPK cascade does not end at p38. Members of the MAPK-activated protein kinase (MAPKAPK) family such as MK2, MK3, and MK5 (PRAK) are all p38 substrates<sup><a href="#ref-3">3</a>,<a href="#ref-4">4</a>,<a href="#ref-53">53</a><a href="#ref-55">55</a></sup>. The MKs have a broad range of substrates that extend the range of functions regulated by p38 kinases. Mitogen- and stress-activated protein kinase-1/2 (MSK1/2), which are important for CREB activation and chromosome remodeling, have also been identified as substrates of p38<sup><a href="#ref-56">56</a></sup>. MNK1/2, kinases that phosphorylate the eukaryotic initiation factor-4e (eIF-4E), are phosphorylated by p38<sup><a href="#ref-57">57</a>,<a href="#ref-58">58</a></sup>. p38 has also been reported to inactivate murine GSK3 by phosphorylating Ser<sup>389</sup>, and since GSK3 is required for the continuous degradation of -catenin in the Wnt signaling pathway, this can lead to an accumulation of -catenin<sup><a href="#ref-59">59</a>,<a href="#ref-60">60</a></sup>. It was also reported that p38 negatively regulates insulin secretion by catalyzing an inhibitory phosphorylation of PKD1<sup><a href="#ref-61">61</a></sup>. A number of p38 protein kinase substrates are summarized in <a href="#T1">Table 1</a>.</p><a name=T1 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 1. id=d15573e849> GSK3, glycogen synthase kinase 3 beta; MAPKAPK, mitogen-activated protein kinase activated protein kinase; MSK1/2, mitogen- and stress-activated protein kinase; PAK6, p21-activated kinase 6; PIP4Kb, phosphatidylinositol 5 phosphate 4-kinase; PKC, protein kinase C epsilon type.</p></div></div></div></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e856 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Transcription factors</h3><p class="" id=d15573e861>p38 targets a large number of transcription factors, including myocyte-specific enhancer factor 2 (MEF2) family members, cyclic AMP-dependent transcription factor 1, 2, and 6 (ATF-1/2/6), CHOP (growth arrest and DNA damage inducible gene 153, or GADD153), p53, C/EBP, MITF1, DDIT3, ELK1/4, NFAT, and STAT1/4. p38 phosphorylation of transcription factors predominantly leads to enhanced transcriptional activity. However, in some cases, it represses transcription, and this is summarized in <a href="#T2">Table 2</a>. Transcription factor phosphorylation by p38 is often stimulus and cell type dependent and plays a role in the cellular response to inflammation, DNA damage, metabolic stress, and many other stresses<sup><a href="#ref-62">62</a><a href="#ref-76">76</a></sup>. The effects of p38 on transcription seem to constitute the major part of p38s responses to stress stimuli.</p><a name=T2 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 2. Substrates of p38 group members  transcription factors.</h3></div><table xmlns:ali="http://www.niso.org/schemas/ali/1.0/" xmlns:mml="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" class=n-a></a><p id=d15573e1836> ATF2, activating transcription factor 2; C/EBP, CCAAT/enhancer binding protein; CHOP, CCAAT/enhancer-binding protein homologous protein; ER, estrogen receptor; GR, glucocorticoid receptor; IUF1, insulin upstream factor 1; JDP2, Jun dimerization protein 2; MEF, myocyte-specific enhancer factor; MITF, microphthalmia transcription factor; MRF, muscle regulatory factor; NFAT, nuclear factor of activated T cells; Pax6, paired box 6; PPAR, peroxisome proliferator-activated receptor alpha; TEAD4, TEA domain family transcription factor 4; USF1, upstream transcription factor 1; Xbp1s, spliced form of X-box binding protein 1.</p></div></div></div></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e1843 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Transcriptional regulators</h3><p class="" id=d15573e1848>A large number of transcriptional regulators, including epigenetic enzymes, are substrates of p38, and these are summarized in <a href="#T3">Table 3</a>. The SWISNF complex subunit BAF60 is phosphorylated and inactivated by p38 during skeletal myogenesis<sup><a href="#ref-109">109</a>,<a href="#ref-110">110</a></sup>, and EZH2, the catalytic component of the Polycomb Repressive Complex 2 (PRC2), was also found to be phosphorylated by p38, particularly in ER-negative breast cancer samples<sup><a href="#ref-111">111</a></sup>. Besides its transcriptional function, dATF-2 is also involved in heterochromatin formation, and stress-induced phosphorylation of dATF-2 by p38 disrupts heterochromatin in <i>Drosophila</i><sup><a href="#ref-112">112</a></sup>.</p><a name=T3 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 3. co-activator 1 alpha; RAR, retinoic acid receptor; RNF2, ring finger protein 2.</p></div></div></div></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e2252 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Other substrates</h3><p class="" id=d15573e2257>Given the wide range of responses that p38 is involved in, it is not surprising that many p38 substrates cannot be so easily categorized into groups, and these miscellaneous substrates are summarized in <a href="#T4">Table 4</a>. Some of them are involved in metabolism such as Raptor phosphorylation by p38, which enhances mTORC1 activity in response to arsenite-stress<sup><a href="#ref-113">113</a></sup>, and DEPTOR (mTOR-inhibitory protein) phosphorylation by p38 and p38, leading to its degradation and mTOR hyperactivation<sup><a href="#ref-114">114</a></sup>. p38 phosphorylation of Tip60 at Thr<sup>158</sup> promotes senescence and DNA-damage-induced apoptosis<sup><a href="#ref-115">115</a>,<a href="#ref-116">116</a></sup>. Some p38 substrates are cell death regulators. In the ER stress response, p38 locates to the lysosome and phosphorylates the chaperone-mediated autophagy (CMA) receptor LAMP2A, leading to activation of CMA and thus protecting cells from ER stress-induced death<sup><a href="#ref-117">117</a></sup>.</p><a name=T4 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 4. rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d15573e3644 class=n-a></a>p38</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d15573e3647 class=n-a></a>Inhibits proteasome activity</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d15573e3650 class=n-a></a>Lee SH <i>et al</i>.<i>, J Biol Chem,</i> 2010<sup><a href="#ref-177">177</a></sup></td></tr><tr><a name=d15573e3662 class=n-a></a><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d15573e3664 class=n-a></a>Siah2</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d15573e3667 class=n-a></a>p38</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d15573e3670 class=n-a></a>Increases Siah2-mediated degradation<br class=br>of PHD3</td><td align=center colspan=1 rowspan=1 valign=top><a name=d15573e3675 class=n-a></a>Khurana A <i>et al</i>.<i>, J Biol Chem,</i> 2006<sup><a href="#ref-178">178</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><div class=table-wrap-foot><div class=footnote><a name=d15573e3691 class=n-a></a><p id=d15573e3693> CDK, cyclin-dependent kinase; EGFR, epidermal growth factor receptor; FBP1, far upstream binding protein; FGF1, fibroblast growth factor 1; FGFR1, fibroblast growth factor receptor 1; FRS2, fibroblast growth factor receptor substrate 2; GDI, GDP dissociation inhibitor; KSRP, hnRNPK-homology type splicing regulatory protein; MAPK, mitogen-activated protein kinase; mTORC1, mammalian target of rapamycin complex 1; NADPH, nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate; NGF, nerve growth factor; NHE1, Na<sup>+</sup>/H<sup>+</sup> exchanger isoform 1; PHD3, prolyl hydroxylase 3; PLA2, phospholipase A2; SAP97, synapse-associated protein 97; TAB, transforming growth factor--activated protein kinase-1-binding protein; TACE, tumor necrosis factor-alpha-converting enzyme; TAK1, transforming growth factor -activated kinase 1; TGF, transforming growth factor.</p></div></div></div></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d15573e3709>Biological functions of the p38 pathway</h2><div class=section><a name=d15573e3712 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Embryo development</h3><p class="" id=d15573e3717>p38 is required for embryo development, since the mouse <i>Mapk14</i><sup>/</sup> embryo dies between embryonic days (E) 10.5 and 12.5<sup><a href="#ref-118">118</a><a href="#ref-121">121</a></sup>. Mutant mice with a single Thr<sup>180</sup> to Ala mutation or with the double T180A Y182F mutation are also embryonic lethal<sup><a href="#ref-122">122</a>,<a href="#ref-123">123</a></sup>. Surprisingly, given the importance of the dual phosphorylation for complete p38 activation, substitution of Tyr<sup>182</sup> with Phe results in mice that have reduced p38 signaling but are nevertheless viable<sup><a href="#ref-123">123</a></sup>, although this is consistent with previous studies showing that the p38 phosphorylated on Thr<sup>180</sup> alone retains some activity <i>in vitro</i><sup><a href="#ref-37">37</a></sup>. Histological analysis demonstrates that p38 is required for placental angiogenesis, but not embryonic cardiovascular development, and tetraploid rescue of the placental defect in <i>Mapk14</i><sup>/</sup> embryos confirmed that p38 is essential for extraembryonic development<sup><a href="#ref-120">120</a>,<a href="#ref-121">121</a></sup>. Given the important role that p38 and MK2 plays in regulating TNF-induced cell death<sup><a href="#ref-179">179</a><a href="#ref-182">182</a></sup>, it is intriguing that the <i>Mapk14</i><sup>/</sup> embryonic lethal phenotype is very similar to that observed in other mice with defects in the TNF death pathway. Caspase-8, FADD, and cFLIP knock-out mice also die at E10.5, and this is due to TNF-dependent endothelial cell death and disruption of the vasculature in the yolk sac<sup><a href="#ref-183">183</a>,<a href="#ref-184">184</a></sup>. Other p38 isoforms are not necessary for embryo development, but p38 and p38 have overlapping functions, as <i>Mapk14<sup>loxp/loxp</sup>Mapk11<sup>/</sup>Sox2-Cre</i> embryos die before E16.5 with spina bifida that correlates with neural hyperproliferation and increased apoptosis in the liver, which was not observed in <i>Mapk14</i><sup>/</sup><i>Sox2-Cre</i> embryos<sup><a href="#ref-185">185</a></sup>. Remarkably, p38 appears to have a very specific function during embryogenesis because when p38 was replaced by p38 in the <i>Mapk14</i> chromosomal locus, which thereby placed p38 under the control of the endogenous p38 promoter, it was unable to rescue the embryonic lethality induced by loss of p38<sup><a href="#ref-185">185</a></sup>.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e3820 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Immune responses</h3><p class="" id=d15573e3825>p38 is activated by many inflammatory stimuli, and its activity is important for inflammatory responses. Macrophage-specific deletion of <i>Mapk14</i> inhibits inflammatory cytokine production and protects mice from CLP-induced sepsis<sup><a href="#ref-186">186</a></sup>. p38 controls the production of inflammatory cytokines, such as TNF and IL-6, at many levels. It directly phosphorylates transcription factors, such as MEF2C<sup><a href="#ref-62">62</a>,<a href="#ref-186">186</a></sup>, and regulators of mRNA stability, such as hnRNPK-homology (KH) type splicing regulatory protein (KSRP)<sup><a href="#ref-187">187</a></sup>. MEF2C appears to play an anti-inflammatory role in endothelial cells <i>in vivo</i><sup><a href="#ref-188">188</a></sup>. Via MK2/MK3, p38 also upregulates cytokine mRNA transcription by the serum response transcription factor (SRF)<sup><a href="#ref-189">189</a></sup>, and similarly, via MK2/MK3, p38 regulates mRNA stability by phosphorylating and inactivating TTP/Zfp36, a protein that promotes rapid turnover of AU-rich mRNAs, many of which are cytokine mRNAs<sup><a href="#ref-187">187</a>,<a href="#ref-190">190</a></sup>. p38 activation also induces the expression of inflammatory mediators such as COX-2, MMP9, iNOS, and VCAM-1, which are involved in tissue remodeling and oxidation regulation<sup><a href="#ref-191">191</a><a href="#ref-194">194</a></sup>. The p38 pathway also regulates adaptive immunity. p38 participates in antigen processing in CD8<sup>+</sup> cDCs<sup><a href="#ref-195">195</a></sup>, and ZAP70-mediated p38/ activation is important for T cell homeostasis and function<sup><a href="#ref-18">18</a></sup>. In B cells, p38 is important for CD40-induced gene expression and proliferation of B cells<sup><a href="#ref-196">196</a></sup>, and the p38MEF2c axis is believed to be necessary for germinal center B (GCB) cell proliferation and survival<sup><a href="#ref-197">197</a>,<a href="#ref-198">198</a></sup>. Excessive activation of p38 has been observed in many inflammatory diseases, such as inflammatory bowel disease (IBD), asthma, rheumatoid arthritis, and steatohepatitis<sup><a href="#ref-199">199</a><a href="#ref-201">201</a></sup>. The other members of the p38 family also play roles in immune responses. For example, p38 and p38 are required for neutrophil migration to damaged liver in non-alcoholic fatty liver disease<sup><a href="#ref-202">202</a></sup> and inhibition of eukaryotic elongation factor 2 in LPS-induced liver damage<sup><a href="#ref-203">203</a></sup>. p38 is required for neutrophil accumulation in acute lung injury<sup><a href="#ref-204">204</a></sup>. These observations, and the role that p38s play in TNF production, led to enormous pharmaceutical efforts to develop p38 inhibitors to treat chronic inflammatory diseases. However, unfortunately, these drugs were not efficacious in these diseases<sup><a href="#ref-205">205</a></sup>.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e3919 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Cell cycle</h3><p class="" id=d15573e3924>p38 has been implicated in G1 and G2/M phases of the cell cycle in several studies. The addition of activated recombinant p38 caused mitotic arrest <i>in vitro</i>, and an inhibitor of p38/ suppressed activation of the checkpoint by nocodazole in NIH3T3 cells<sup><a href="#ref-206">206</a></sup>. G1 arrest caused by Cdc42 overexpression is also dependent on p38 in NIH3T3 cells<sup><a href="#ref-207">207</a></sup>. Besides, p38 is specially required for gamma-irradiation-induced G2 arrest<sup><a href="#ref-208">208</a></sup>. The link between p38 and cell cycle control has been proposed through the regulation of several p38 substrates. Both p38 and p38 regulate cell cycle progression via Rb but in opposite directions<sup><a href="#ref-14">14</a>,<a href="#ref-209">209</a></sup>. HBP1 represses the expression of cell cycle regulatory genes during cell cycle arrest in a p38-dependent manner<sup><a href="#ref-210">210</a></sup>; p53 and p21 activation by p38 prevented G1 progression through blockade of CDK activity<sup><a href="#ref-211">211</a>,<a href="#ref-212">212</a></sup>. The p38 pathway is also involved in cell cycle progress, as it is essential for self-renewal of mouse male germline stem cells<sup><a href="#ref-213">213</a></sup> and its regulation of G1-length plays a role in cell size uniformity<sup><a href="#ref-214">214</a></sup>.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e3970 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Cell differentiation</h3><p class="" id=d15573e3975>Participation of p38 in cell differentiation has been reported in certain cell types. p38 activity is essential for neuronal differentiation in PC-12 cells and EPO-induced differentiation in SKT6 cells<sup><a href="#ref-20">20</a>,<a href="#ref-215">215</a></sup>. Treatment of 3T3-L1 fibroblasts with specific p38/ inhibitors prevents their differentiation into adipocytes by reducing C/EBP phosphorylation<sup><a href="#ref-83">83</a></sup>, and p38-dependent phosphorylation of MEF2C and BAF60 is critical for myogenic differentiation<sup><a href="#ref-110">110</a>,<a href="#ref-216">216</a></sup>. Intestinal epithelial cell-specific deletion of p38 also influences goblet cell differentiation in a Notch-dependent manner<sup><a href="#ref-200">200</a></sup>.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e4002 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Cell metabolism</h3><p class="" id=d15573e4007>p38 group members participate in many cellular events related to metabolism. The p38PRAK axis specifically phosphorylates Rheb and suppresses mTORC1 activity under energy depletion conditions<sup><a href="#ref-22">22</a></sup>. DEPTOR, an inhibitor of mTORC, can be phosphorylated by p38 and p38, leading to its degradation<sup><a href="#ref-123">123</a></sup>. Meanwhile, p38 directly phosphorylated p62 to enhance mTORC1 activity in response to amino acids<sup><a href="#ref-175">175</a></sup>. In brown adipocytes, p38 functions as a central mediator in -adrenergic-induced UCP1 expression<sup><a href="#ref-217">217</a>,<a href="#ref-218">218</a></sup>, while in white adipocytes, p38 inactivation leads to elevated white-to-beige adipocyte reprogramming and resistance to diet-induced obesity<sup><a href="#ref-219">219</a>,<a href="#ref-220">220</a></sup>. In hepatocytes, p38 controls lipolysis and protects against nutritional steatohepatitis. Thus, mice with hepatocyte-specific loss of p38 developed more severe steatohepatitis than wild type mice when fed high-fat or -cholesterol diets. Intriguingly, macrophage specific deletion of p38 had the opposite effect in the same high-fat diets and resulted in less steatohepatitis than in wild type mice, which probably reflects the inflammatory role of p38 in macrophages<sup><a href="#ref-199">199</a></sup>. p38 also directly phosphorylates Xbp1s to enhance its nuclear migration for maintaining glucose homeostasis in obesity<sup><a href="#ref-75">75</a></sup>. However, p38 also functions as a negative regulator of AMPK signaling in maintaining gluconeogenesis, and hepatic p38 could be a drug target for hyperglycemia<sup><a href="#ref-221">221</a></sup>. It was also reported that p38 directly phosphorylated p62 under imidazole propionate stimulation to promote mTORC1 activity in hepatocytes<sup><a href="#ref-176">176</a></sup>. Interestingly, AMPK also triggers the recruitment of p38 to scaffold protein TAB1 for p38 autoactivation in human T cells<sup><a href="#ref-222">222</a></sup>.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e4058 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Cell senescence</h3><p class="" id=d15573e4063>p38 appears to play a pivotal role in senescence. Constitutive activation of the p38 pathway by active MKK3 or MKK6 induces senescence in several cell types<sup><a href="#ref-223">223</a>,<a href="#ref-224">224</a></sup>, and p38 activity is responsible for senescence induced by multiple stimuli, such as telomere shortening<sup><a href="#ref-225">225</a>,<a href="#ref-226">226</a></sup>, H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> exposure<sup><a href="#ref-227">227</a>,<a href="#ref-228">228</a></sup>, and chronic oncogene activation<sup><a href="#ref-19">19</a>,<a href="#ref-223">223</a>,<a href="#ref-229">229</a></sup>. p38/-specific inhibitors have been successfully used to prevent cellular senescence in cultivated human corneal endothelial cells<sup><a href="#ref-230">230</a></sup>. Since cellular senescence is considered a defense strategy against oncogene activation, the p38 pathway plays important roles in tumorigenesis<sup><a href="#ref-231">231</a></sup>. Meanwhile, p38 activity is important for senescence-associated secretory phenotype (SASP), and its inhibition markedly reduces the secretion of most SASP factors, suggesting multiple roles for the p38 pathway in senescence<sup><a href="#ref-232">232</a><a href="#ref-235">235</a></sup>.</p></div><div class=section><a name=d15573e4120 class=n-a></a><h3 class=section-title>Cell survival and death</h3><p class="" id=d15573e4125>The role of the p38 pathway in cell fate is cell type and stimulus dependent. For example, p38 becomes activated upon NGF withdrawal in PC-12 cells, and p38 activated by overexpression of MKK3 induced apoptosis in NGF differentiated PC-12 cells<sup><a href="#ref-211">211</a></sup>. Similarly, inhibition of p38 with PD169316 blocked NGF withdrawal-induced apoptosis in PC-12 cells<sup><a href="#ref-236">236</a>,<a href="#ref-237">237</a></sup>. The interplay between the p38 pathway and caspases, the central regulators/executors of apoptosis, is complicated because p38 activity can be elevated in a caspase-dependent manner in death stimulus treated cells<sup><a href="#ref-238">238</a>,<a href="#ref-239">239</a></sup>, and caspase activity can also be elevated in MKK6E (dominant active form) overexpressed cells<sup><a href="#ref-239">239</a>,<a href="#ref-240">240</a></sup>. In contrast, inhibition of caspase-8 and caspase-3 by p38-mediated phosphorylation in neutrophils was also reported<sup><a href="#ref-140">140</a></sup>. Recent studies show that p38-activated MK2 directly phosphorylates RIPK1 in TNF-treated cells or pathogen-infected cells, limiting TNF-induced cell death<sup><a href="#ref-180">180</a><a href="#ref-182">182</a></sup>. This represents an interesting link between cytokine production induced by TNF and cell death because TNF-induced MK2/MK3 phosphorylation of tristetraprolin/Zfp36 inactivates it and leads to increased stability of cytokine mRNAs<sup><a href="#ref-190">190</a></sup>. Aberrant p38 activity is observed in many tumor cells, and inhibition of p38/ enhances cell death in these cells<sup><a href="#ref-241">241</a>,<a href="#ref-242">242</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div id=article1-body class=generated-article-body><h2 class=main-title id=d15573e4180>Perspectives</h2><p class="" id=d15573e4183>p38 is one of the most researched of all proteins, let alone kinases, and a search in PubMed for p38 MAPK or p38 kinase returns more than 36,000 publications, which is a higher number than some proteins listed in a review of the "top 10" most studied genes<sup><a href="#ref-243">243</a></sup>. By contrast, searches for the kinases Raf and Src return about 17,000 and 25,000 hits, respectively. In 2018, there were more than 2,000 publications that mention p38, and it is clearly impractical to summarize such a vast amount of literature. As might be surmised from the preceding commentary, the studies are on a wide range of topics; however, the publications are more concentrated in some areas than others. The role of the p38 pathway in cancers (&gt;10,000)<sup><a href="#ref-244">244</a><a href="#ref-246">246</a></sup>, inflammation (&gt;8,000)<sup><a href="#ref-247">247</a><a href="#ref-249">249</a></sup>, and infections (&gt;3,600)<sup><a href="#ref-250">250</a>,<a href="#ref-251">251</a></sup> was intensively studied. About 1,600 publications include the specific term "p38 inhibitor". This reflects the previously mentioned enormous interest of the pharmaceutical industry in developing p38 inhibitors to treat chronic inflammatory diseases, such as rheumatoid arthritis. Yet other publications report natural products that can activate or inhibit p38, with the ultimate aim of using them clinically<sup><a href="#ref-252">252</a><a href="#ref-258">258</a></sup>. In 2011, the European Commission approved Esbriet (pirfenidone), which was described as a p38 inhibitor, for the treatment of idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis<sup><a href="#ref-259">259</a></sup>. However, when this drug was approved by the FDA in 2014 for treating the same disease, it was described as a compound that acts on multiple pathways. In 2008, there were 27 clinical trials listed testing the use of p38 inhibitors in inflammatory disease settings<sup><a href="#ref-205">205</a></sup>, while a search today for p38 inhibitors in clinicaltrials.gov returns 44 studies for conditions as diverse as pain, asthma, cognitive impairment, rheumatoid arthritis, cancer, myelodysplastic syndrome, and depression (<a href="#T5">Table 5</a>). This indicates that there remains clinical interest in targeting the pathway and that there is therefore a need for more specific inhibitors of each of the p38 group members and more basic research to fully understand how the pathway, especially how each member of the p38 family, is utilized and regulated.</p><a name=T5 class=n-a></a><div class="table-wrap panel clearfix"><div class=caption><h3>Table 5. colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d15573e5026 class=n-a></a>VX-702</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d15573e5029 class=n-a></a>p38</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d15573e5032 class=n-a></a>Rheumatoid arthritis</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d15573e5035 class=n-a></a>Phase 2</td><td align=left colspan=1 rowspan=1><a name=d15573e5038 class=n-a></a>NCT00395577<br class=br>NCT00205478</td></tr></tbody></table></div><p class="" id=d15573e5047>One consequence of the massive pharmaceutical effort over the last 20 years is a large number of very specific, well-tolerated, and readily bioavailable drugs that can enable such basic research. For example, one study using a boutique panel of kinase inhibitors was able to demonstrate that 11 potent and specific p38 inhibitors synergized with Smac-mimetic drugs to kill a subset of AML leukemias, providing the strongest evidence implicating p38 in Smac-mimetic-induced killing<sup><a href="#ref-179">179</a></sup>. Since several of these p38 inhibitors had already been clinically trialed, this presents an opportunity to fast-track such combinations into the clinic. In our opinion, it is likely that this is where the future of p38 research and p38 inhibitors lies, in revealing the intricate web of inter-connections and inter-dependencies of this core and central regulator of cell stress. We also believe that greater efforts to genetically assess the role of p38 and p38 isoforms in the pathophysiology of inflammatory and other diseases need to be made in order to push forward the clinical application of our burgeoning knowledge.</p></div><div id=article1-back class=generated-article-footer><div class=back-section><a name=d15573e5058 class=n-a></a><h2 class=main-title id=d15573e5060>Acknowledgments</h2><p class="" id=d15573e5063>We thank Peipei Zhang and Ye-hsuan Sun (School of Life Sciences, Xiamen University, China) for their help in preparing this manuscript.</p></div><div class=back-section><a name=d15573e5067 class=n-a></a><span class="research-layout prime-recommended-wrapper reference-heading is-hidden"><span class="f1r-icon icon-79_faculty_recommended_badge red vmiddle default-cursor"></span><span class="prime-red big">F1000 recommended</span></span><h2 class=main-title id=d17312>References</h2><div class="section ref-list"><a name=d15573e5067 class=n-a></a><ul><li><a name=ref-1 class=n-a></a><span class=label>1. </span>&nbsp;<span class=citation><a name=d15573e5074 class=n-a></a>Han J, Lee JD, Tobias PS, <i> et al.</i>: Endotoxin induces rapid protein tyrosine phosphorylation in 70Z/3 cells expressing CD14. <i>J Biol Chem.</i> 1993; </span> <div class=editorial-note> <h5 class=editorial-note__title>Editorial Note on the Review Process</h5> <p class=editorial-note__text><a href="/browse/faculty-reviews">Faculty Reviews</a> are review articles written by the prestigious Members of <a href="https://facultyopinions.com/prime/home">Faculty Opinions</a>. The articles are commissioned and peer reviewed before publication to ensure that the final, published version is comprehensive and accessible. The reviewers who approved the final version are listed with their names and affiliations.</p> </div> <div class=approved-referee> <h4 class=approved-referee__title>Reviewers who approved this article</h4> <ol class=approved-referee__list> <li class="approved-referee__list-item "> <span class=approved-referee__co-referee> <strong>Jonathan D. class="f1r-icon icon-86_approved status-green smaller" title=Approved></span> <span class=research-help-text>Approved - the paper is scientifically sound in its current form and only minor, if any, improvements are suggested</span> </div> <div class="cf research-help-row"> <span class="f1r-icon icon-87_approved_reservations status-green smaller" title="Approved with Reservations"></span> <span Consider the following examples, but note that this is not an exhaustive list: </p> <div class=heading5>Examples of 'Non-Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>Within the past 4 years, you have held joint grants, published or collaborated with any of the authors of the selected paper.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You have a close personal relationship (e.g. parent, spouse, sibling, or domestic partner) with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are a close professional associate of any of the authors (e.g. scientific mentor, recent student).</li> <li class=standard-padding>You work at the same institute as any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hope/expect to benefit (e.g. favour or employment) as a result of your submission.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are an Editor for the journal in which the article is published.</li> </ol> <div class="heading5 padding-top">Examples of 'Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, any of the following from any commercial organisation that may gain financially from your submission: a salary, fees, funding, reimbursements.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, shared grant support or other funding with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hold, or are currently applying for, any patents or significant stocks/shares relating to the subject matter of the paper you are commenting on.</li> </ol> </div> <div class="f1r-article-mobile research-layout"> <span class=modal-window-close-button></span> </div> </div> </div> <div scientific mentor, recent student).</li> <li class=standard-padding>You work at the same institute as any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hope/expect to benefit (e.g. favour or employment) as a result of your submission.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You are an Editor for the journal in which the article is published.</li> </ol> <div class="heading5 padding-top">Examples of 'Financial Competing Interests'</div> <ol class="numbered-list no-padding"> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, any of the following from any commercial organisation that may gain financially from your submission: a salary, fees, funding, reimbursements.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You expect to receive, or in the past 4 years have received, shared grant support or other funding with any of the authors.</li> <li class=standard-padding>You hold, or are currently applying for, any patents or significant stocks/shares relating to
